But then we hit Seven of Wands, and suddenly you're defending your creative choices to everyone. Your mom's asking when you're going to "get a real job," your friends are side-eyeing your freelance schedule, and you're constantly having to explain why yes, you ARE working even though you're in your pajamas at 2 PM editing podcast episodes.
Eight of Wands is when everything accelerates. Orders are flying in, clients want everything yesterday, your DMs are flooded, and you're trying to keep up with demand while simultaneously trying not to burn out from the success you've been manifesting for months.
Ten of Wands? That's when your passion project becomes the thing that's actually exhausting you. When you realize that turning your creative outlet into your income source means it's not really an outlet anymore. It's when "do what you love and you'll never work a day in your life" reveals itself to be a beautiful lie, because you absolutely are working, just without the safety net of traditional employment benefits.
The Court Cards in Wands are your creative collaborators and competitors. Page of Wands is that person who just started their YouTube channel and has boundless enthusiasm but zero business sense. Knight of Wands launched three different ventures this year and abandoned two of them. Queen of Wands is running a successful creative business and making it look effortless. King of Wands owns multiple creative companies and somehow still finds time to mentor other creators.
Wands in the gig economy remind us that creative work is still work, even when you love it. Especially when you love it.
Cups: The Emotional Labor Economy
Now let's talk about Cups, because if you're in any kind of service work, care work, emotional labor, or any job where you have to manage other people's feelings for money, you're swimming in Cups energy all day long.
This includes obvious stuff like therapy, counseling, nursing, teaching, social work. But it also includes the hidden emotional labor in jobs that don't acknowledge it: customer service representatives who have to stay cheerful while being screamed at, baristas who have to perform friendship with every latte, rideshare drivers who become unpaid therapists for drunk passengers at 2 AM.
Two of Cups in modern work is about connection and collaboration. It's finding your work spouse, your creative partner, that person who gets your professional vision and helps you build it. In gig work, it's finding clients who actually value what you do, who pay on time, who treat you like a human being instead of a service robot.
Three of Cups is team energy. It's finding your professional community, your business bestie group chat, the other freelancers who understand why you're celebrating landing a project that pays $500 like you just won the lottery. It's networking that doesn't feel gross because it's actually just friendship with people who happen to share your professional struggles.
But Five of Cups shows up when the emotional labor becomes too much. When you realize you've been managing everyone else's feelings at the expense of your own. When you've given so much emotional energy to clients or customers or collaborators that you have nothing left for yourself. This card appears when you need to mourn the boundaries you should have set six months ago.
Seven of Cups is choice paralysis in the emotion-based economy. When you have seventeen different service options you could offer, twelve different ways to package your expertise, and you're paralyzed by trying to figure out what people actually want versus what you're actually good at providing.
Nine of Cups is the satisfaction card, the "I actually love what I do" moment. It's when a client sends you a thank-you note that makes you remember why you started this work. It's getting paid well for work that actually matters to you. It's the reminder that yes, you can make money doing work that feeds your soul.
The emotional labor economy is real, and Cups cards help us navigate it without completely burning out our empathy reserves.
Swords: Communication, Information, and Mental Labor
Swords energy runs through every job that's primarily mental labor: writing, consulting, research, analysis, strategy, communication. If your work happens primarily in your head or through your words, you're working in Swords territory.
This is the realm of content creators, copywriters, consultants, researchers, analysts, lawyers, journalists, anyone whose primary tool is their mind and their ability to communicate ideas clearly. It's also the energy behind the mental gymnastics of gig work logisticsŚtracking expenses, managing multiple clients, negotiating rates, handling your own marketing.
Ace of Swords is clarity cutting through confusion. It's the moment you figure out exactly what service you want to offer, what message you want to communicate, what problem you're actually solving for people. It's breakthrough thinking, the idea that changes everything.
Two of Swords is decision paralysis about business direction. Should you specialize or stay generalist? Raise your rates or keep them accessible? Take on that big project that feels slightly outside your expertise? It's standing at the crossroads of professional choices, knowing that each decision closes other doors.
Three of Swords is the heartbreak of professional betrayal. Clients who don't pay, collaborators who steal your ideas, projects that fall apart after you've invested months of work. It's the specific pain of having your intellectual labor devalued or exploited.
Five of Swords is about winning arguments you shouldn't have had to make. Defending your rates, your processes, your professional boundaries. It's the exhaustion that comes from constantly having to prove your expertise instead of just being allowed to do the work.
Seven of Swords is about strategy and sometimes necessary professional cunning. Protecting your intellectual property, being strategic about what information you share with potential competitors, knowing how to position yourself in a crowded market without giving away all your secrets for free.
Ten of Swords is intellectual burnout. When your brain feels like it's been wrung out like a dishrag, when you can't form one more coherent thought, when the idea of writing another email makes you want to fake your own death and become a forest hermit.
Swords remind us that mental labor is still labor, even when it doesn't leave visible evidence of effort.
Pentacles: The Material Reality of Making Money
Finally, PentaclesŚthe suit that's all about tangible results, material success, actual money in actual bank accounts. This is where the rubber meets the road in modern work. You can have all the creative passion, emotional intelligence, and brilliant ideas in the world, but Pentacles energy asks the practical question: "Okay, but how are you paying rent?"
Pentacles in the gig economy cover everything from freelancing rates to investment strategies, from tracking business expenses to building actual wealth instead of just staying afloat. It's the energy of treating your work like a business, even when your office is your kitchen table and your business meeting is a Zoom call in your bedroom.
Ace of Pentacles is a new opportunity for material success. A new client, a rate increase, a business idea that has actual profit potential. It's tangible opportunity you can build on.
Two of Pentacles is the juggling act of multiple income streams. Balancing your day job with your side hustle, managing feast-or-famine client cycles, trying to grow one business while maintaining another. It's the beautiful chaos of modern work diversification.
Three of Pentacles is collaboration that produces tangible results. Working with others in a way that creates real value, real income, real professional growth. It's the energy behind successful business partnerships and profitable collaborative projects.
Four of Pentacles is about financial security, but sometimes it shows up as being too conservative with business investments or too scared to raise your rates. It's the tension between financial safety and business growth.
Six of Pentacles is about fair exchangeŚpaying others well for their work, charging appropriately for your own, creating business relationships that benefit everyone involved. It's moving beyond exploitation toward genuine professional reciprocity.
Seven of Pentacles is the patience required for long-term business building. Investing time and energy now for results that won't show up for months or years. It's the energy needed for sustainable business growth instead of quick cash grabs.
Ten of Pentacles is wealth-building energy, creating systems that generate ongoing income instead of just trading time for money. It's thinking generationally about your work, building something that lasts beyond immediate survival needs.
Section II: Major Arcana in Career Transitions
Let's talk about the Major Arcana cards that show up most often when people are making big career moves, because honestly, if you're not having at least one major career crisis per year at this point, you're probably not paying attention to how fast everything is changing.
The Fool shows up when you're about to do something completely terrifying and necessary for your career. Quitting your job to freelance full-time. Starting a business with no guarantee it'll work. Applying for positions you're not technically qualified for yet. Moving across the country for a job opportunity. The Fool is pure audacity in professional form, and sometimes audacity is exactly what's required to break out of career stagnation.
You know you're in Fool energy when you catch yourself saying things like "I have no idea what I'm doing, but I'm doing it anyway" or "I can't afford to do this, but I can't afford not to do this." The Fool doesn't have a backup plan because backup plans can become excuses to avoid taking the leap that needs to be taken.
The Magician is about building multiple income streams and having all the tools you need to succeed. This card shows up when you realize you actually have more skills and resources than you thought. The Magician takes inventory: What can I offer? What do people need? How can I combine my existing abilities in new ways?
In gig economy terms, The Magician is that moment when you realize your nursing background, your tech skills, and your tarot knowledge can all work together instead of competing for time and attention. It's recognizing that your diverse experience is actually an asset, not a liability.
The Chariot is about successfully managing multiple professional directions at once. If you're juggling a day job, a side hustle, and a passion project, Chariot energy helps you harness all that motion and point it in a productive direction. The horses want to go different ways, but the charioteer knows how to work with that tension instead of fighting it.
This card appears when you need to get serious about project management for your own life. When you need systems, schedules, and boundaries to keep all your professional ventures moving forward without crashing into each other.
Death is the career pivot card. Something about your professional identity needs to die so something new can be born. Maybe it's the version of yourself that stays in jobs you've outgrown because the security feels safer than the unknown. Maybe it's the story you tell about what you're "supposed" to be doing with your life.
Death shows up before major career transitions, and it's never comfortable. But it's also mercifulŚit ends things that were already draining your life force, even when you weren't ready to admit they needed to end.
The Tower is when external circumstances force career changes you weren't prepared for. Company layoffs, industry disruption, pandemic-related job losses, technology making your entire profession obsolete. The Tower doesn't ask if you're ready for changeŚit just demolishes structures that were already unstable.
But here's the thing about Tower moments in careers: they often force you into opportunities you were too scared to pursue voluntarily. Sometimes getting fired from a job you hated is exactly the push you needed to start the business you'd been talking about for three years.
The Star represents sustainable success, the career version of finding your calling and actually being able to make a living from it. This card appears when you've weathered the transitions and found work that feeds both your soul and your bank account.
Star energy in modern careers is about building something that serves others while serving yourself. It's creating value in the world while creating security for your future. It's the sustainable version of professional success that doesn't require you to sacrifice your health, relationships, or values.
The World is career integrationŚwhen all the pieces of your professional life come together in a way that makes sense. When your day job supports your creative work, when your side hustle complements your main career, when everything you've learned and experienced contributes to a professional identity that feels authentically you.
This doesn't mean you only do one thingŚit means all the things you do work together instead of competing with each other. It's the completion of a professional cycle that sets you up for the next level of growth.
Section III: Career Spreads for Modern Work
Alright, let's get practical. Here are some spreads specifically designed for the realities of modern work life. These aren't about predicting your future careerŚthey're about clarifying your current situation so you can make better decisions.
The "Should I Quit My Day Job?" Spread
This is a three-card spread that cuts straight through the fantasy and gets to the practical reality of major career transitions.
Card 1: What you're really leaving behind This isn't just about salary and benefits. What professional growth opportunities are you walking away from? What skills are you not developing? What connections are you not making? What security are you giving up? Be honest about the full cost of leaving.
Card 2: What you're really moving toward This also isn't just about following your passion. What specific opportunities are available in your new direction? What skills will you develop? What kind of income timeline is realistic? What are you actually gaining, not just what you hope to gain?
Card 3: What you need to bridge the gap What financial cushion do you need? What skills should you develop before you make the jump? What connections should you make? What systems need to be in place? This card shows you the practical steps required to make the transition successfully instead of just hopefully.
I pulled this spread before leaving my ICU position to focus more on utilization review and tech projects. The first card was The Ten of Pentacles reversedŚI was leaving behind financial security and the identity that came with being "an ICU nurse." The second card was The MagicianŚI was moving toward using all my skills in new combinations. The third card was The Four of PentaclesŚI needed to be strategic about money and not make the jump until I had more financial stability built up.
Did I listen to the cards? Mostly. Did I still make the jump a little earlier than I probably should have? Obviously. But having that clarity helped me prepare better than I would have otherwise.
Multiple Income Streams Assessment
This is a five-card spread for when you're managing multiple professional projects and need to figure out where to focus your energy.
Card 1: Main income source - current state How stable is your primary income? What's working well? What needs attention? This card gives you perspective on your foundation.
Card 2: Side hustle - potential and problems What opportunities exist in your secondary income streams? What obstacles are holding them back? What do you need to know about developing this area?
Card 3: Passion project - reality check If you have creative work or passion projects, this card shows you their actual potential versus your fantasy about their potential. Be prepared for some harsh truths here.
Card 4: Where to invest your time and energy Of all your professional activities, where will effort pay off most? What deserves your attention right now? What should you be prioritizing?
Card 5: What to release or put on hold What professional activities are draining energy without providing adequate return? What projects should you pause or abandon to focus on what's actually working?
This spread is particularly useful when you're feeling scattered across too many projects. Sometimes you need permission to stop doing things that aren't serving you, even when they seemed like good ideas when you started them.
Work-Life Balance Check
This is a seven-card spread arranged in a specific pattern that examines how your work life and personal life are actually affecting each other.
Cards 1-3: Work's impact on your life
* Card 1: How your work affects your relationships
* Card 2: How your work affects your health and energy
* Card 3: How your work affects your personal growth and happiness
Cards 4-6: Life's impact on your work
* Card 4: How your personal relationships affect your professional performance
* Card 5: How your physical and mental health affects your work capacity
* Card 6: How your personal values align with your professional choices
Card 7: Integration strategy What would actual balance look like for you? What changes would create better integration between work and life? This card shows you a specific action or mindset shift that would improve both areas.
The truth is, work-life balance is kind of a myth when you're running multiple income streams or building a business. It's more about work-life integrationŚmaking sure all the pieces of your life support each other instead of competing for resources.
When I did this spread last month, the integration card was The Hermit, which told me I needed to actually take the time to think strategically about my professional choices instead of just reacting to opportunities as they appeared. Sometimes the answer to overwhelm is not doing more efficientlyŚit's doing less, but more intentionally.
These spreads aren't magic. They're structured ways of thinking through complex situations using the tarot's framework for understanding different types of energy and motivation. They work because they force you to consider aspects of your situation you might otherwise ignore or rationalize away.
The modern career path isn't a ladder anymoreŚit's more like a jungle gym. You can move in any direction, but you need to be intentional about where you're going and why. The cards can help you figure out which direction serves your actual goals instead of just your fears or fantasies.
And sometimes, honestly, the cards will tell you to stay exactly where you are and stop overthinking everything. Which is also useful information, even when it's not what you wanted to hear.
The gig economy has its benefitsŚflexibility, variety, the possibility of building something entirely your own. But it also requires you to be your own HR department, your own business development team, your own financial planner, and your own career counselor. The tarot can't replace professional expertise in any of those areas, but it can help you think through the decisions you have to make in all of them.
Because at the end of the day, whether you're building a business or climbing a corporate ladder or creating some hybrid of traditional and freelance work, you're still just a human trying to exchange your time and skills for enough money to live the life you want. The cards just help you be more honest about what that life actually looks like and what it actually costs.


Chapter 18: Career Spreads for Real-World Decisions

Look, I'm going to be straight with you about something: most tarot spreads for career questions are about as useful as a chocolate teapot. They're either so vague they could apply to literally anyone ("focus on your strengths, trust your intuition") or so mystical they sound like they were written by someone who's never actually had to choose between paying rent and following their dreams.
But here's the thingŚwhen you're standing at a professional crossroads at 2 AM, stress-scrolling through job postings while your cat judges you for eating cereal for dinner again, you don't need cosmic wisdom about your soul's purpose. You need practical clarity about actual decisions you have to make with actual consequences.
So that's what these spreads are designed to do. They're not going to tell you what the universe wants for your career. They're going to help you figure out what YOU want, what you're actually dealing with, and what concrete steps might actually help. Because sometimes the most spiritual thing you can do is pay your bills on time and stop romanticizing financial stress.
I've been reading cards for people's work situations for over two decades now, and I've noticed that the same questions keep coming up. Not "what's my purpose" (though that's valid), but "should I take this job that pays more but feels soul-crushing?" and "how do I ask for a raise without sounding like an entitled millennial?" and "is freelancing actually viable or am I just procrastinating on real life?"
These spreads address those specific, practical questions. They're designed for people who need to make decisions next week, not someday when the stars align.
1. "Is This Job Right for Me?" Spread
This six-card spread is for when you have a specific job opportunityŚwhether it's a position you're interviewing for, considering applying to, or already have but are questioning. It cuts through the excitement of potential change and the fear of making mistakes to look at the actual fit.
Position 1 (Bottom center): The core reality of this role What is this job actually offering beyond what's written in the description? What's the day-to-day energy? What are you really signing up for?
Position 2 (Left): What you bring to this position Your strengths, skills, and qualities that make you suitable. Sometimes this reveals capabilities you hadn't considered or reminds you of assets you've been undervaluing.
Position 3 (Right): What this position brings to you Beyond salary and titleŚwhat growth, connections, skills, or opportunities does this role actually offer? What do you gain?
Position 4 (Top left): Potential challenges or red flags What problems might you encounter? What should you be asking about in interviews? What could go wrong?
Position 5 (Top right): Growth and opportunity potential Where could this job lead? What doors might it open? How does it fit into your longer-term career trajectory?
Position 6 (Top center): Decision guidance The overall energy around taking or declining this opportunity. What should you prioritize in making this choice?
Sample Reading:
I did this spread recently for someone considering a marketing role at a startup. Here's what came up:
Core reality (Four of Pentacles): The company was more financially unstable than they were letting on. Resource constraints, penny-pinching, probably delayed paychecks.
What you bring (Queen of Swords): Sharp communication skills, strategic thinking, ability to cut through startup BS and get things done efficiently.
What position brings you (Ace of Wands): Genuine creative freedom, chance to build something from scratch, opportunity to see immediate impact of your work.
Challenges (Seven of Swords): Be careful about verbal promises that don't make it into your contract. Get everything in writing. Trust but verify.
Growth potential (Three of Pentacles): Strong collaborative opportunities, chance to work directly with founders, potential for significant role expansion.
Decision guidance (Two of Pentacles): This job requires juggling multiple priorities and financial uncertainty. Only take it if you can handle the instability for the sake of the experience.
Action Steps:
* Ask detailed questions about company finances and runway
* Negotiate for equity or performance bonuses to offset salary risk
* Set a timeline for how long you're willing to stay if growth doesn't materialize
* Make sure you have enough savings to weather potential payment delays
The person took the job but negotiated a higher salary and stock options, and set a one-year evaluation point. Sometimes the cards don't tell you yes or noŚthey tell you how to make whatever choice you're leaning toward work better.
2. "Should I Take This Opportunity?" Spread
This five-card spread is broader than the job-specific oneŚit works for any professional opportunity, from freelance projects to speaking engagements to business partnerships. It's designed to cut through FOMO and fear to look at actual strategic value.
Position 1 (Center): The opportunity's true nature What is this really about? What are you actually being offered beyond the surface description?
Position 2 (Left): What you sacrifice by saying yes Time, energy, other opportunities, money, relationshipsŚwhat's the real cost of saying yes?
Position 3 (Right): What you sacrifice by saying no What do you miss out on? What doors might close? What are you giving up by declining?
Position 4 (Top): Long-term impact How does this opportunity affect your trajectory? Where does it lead? What does it set you up for?
Position 5 (Bottom): Your gut wisdom What does your intuition know that your logical mind is missing? What are you not considering?
Sample Reading:
A colleague asked about a speaking opportunity at a major conferenceŚgreat exposure but no pay and she'd have to cover travel costs.
True nature (Three of Cups): Networking gold mine. The value isn't the speaking itself, it's the connections you'll make and the credibility boost.
Cost of yes (Five of Pentacles): Financial strain from travel costs, time away from paying work, energy drain from travel and performance.
Cost of no (Eight of Wands): Missing a window of opportunity. This conference might not invite you back if you decline this time.
Long-term impact (Six of Pentacles): Sets you up as a thought leader in your field. Future paid opportunities will come from this exposure.
Gut wisdom (The Hermit): You need this professional challenge to grow, even though the practical side of you wants to decline.
Action Steps:
* Accept the opportunity but reach out to other attendees beforehand to maximize networking value
* Apply for partial travel reimbursement or find sponsors
* Book additional meetings in that city to maximize the trip's value
* Use this speaking engagement to book future paid speaking opportunities
She took the opportunity and it led to two consulting contracts and a board position within six months. Sometimes investments in professional visibility pay off in ways you can't predict.
3. "Freelance vs. Full-Time" Spread
This seven-card spread is for the eternal modern dilemma: traditional employment security versus freelance freedom. It's designed to get past romantic notions about "being your own boss" and practical fears about "unstable income" to look at what each path actually offers YOU specifically.
Position 1 (Center): Your current professional reality Where you are now, what's working, what isn't, what you need to change.
Position 2 (Left): Freelance path - benefits What freelancing would give you that employment doesn't. Freedom, variety, control, income potential.
Position 3 (Far left): Freelance path - challenges Irregular income, self-discipline requirements, business management responsibilities, isolation, benefits costs.
Position 4 (Right): Employment path - benefits Stability, benefits, team collaboration, clear boundaries, predictable schedule, learning opportunities.
Position 5 (Far right): Employment path - challenges Limited autonomy, office politics, income ceiling, less flexibility, potential for getting stuck.
Position 6 (Top): What you really need right now Given your life circumstances, personality, and goals, what matters most in your work situation?
Position 7 (Bottom): Timeline and transition guidance How and when to make whatever change you're considering. What steps to take first.
Sample Reading:
I pulled this for myself when deciding whether to go full freelance or stay in traditional nursing roles:
Current reality (The Hanged Man): Feeling stuck between two worlds, unable to fully commit to either traditional healthcare or business ventures.
Freelance benefits (The Magician): Full use of all my skillsŚclinical, technical, creative. Income potential from multiple streams.
Freelance challenges (Five of Pentacles): Financial insecurity, especially losing healthcare benefits. Discipline required for business development.
Employment benefits (Four of Pentacles): Steady paycheck, benefits, clear boundaries between work and personal time.
Employment challenges (Eight of Swords): Feeling trapped by other people's schedules and priorities, limited growth potential in traditional roles.
What I need (Six of Pentacles): Balance. Fair exchange of time for money, work that serves others while serving my goals.
Transition guidance (Two of Pentacles): Gradual transition. Build freelance income while maintaining some traditional work. Test the waters before diving in completely.
Action Steps:
* Reduce traditional work to part-time or per-diem
* Build freelance client base gradually
* Maintain healthcare through spouse's plan or marketplace
* Set specific income benchmarks before reducing traditional work further
* Create systems for business management and financial tracking
The cards basically told me to stop thinking in either/or terms and create a hybrid approach, which is exactly what I ended up doing. Sometimes the answer isn't choosing between two optionsŚit's finding a third option that combines the best of both.
4. "Asking for a Raise" Spread
This four-card spread is specifically for when you need to have that awkward money conversation with your boss. It helps you prepare strategically instead of just hoping for the best or avoiding the conversation entirely because it feels uncomfortable.
Position 1 (Bottom): Your current value and contributions What you actually bring to the organization. Your strengths, achievements, and impact that justify increased compensation.
Position 2 (Left): Obstacles and objections What resistance might you encounter? Budget constraints, company policies, your boss's perspective, timing issues.
Position 3 (Right): Opportunities and leverage What's working in your favor? Market conditions, company performance, your irreplaceability, timing advantages.
Position 4 (Top): Strategy and approach How to frame the conversation, what to emphasize, when to have it, what alternatives to propose.
Sample Reading:
A friend used this spread before asking for a promotion and raise after covering additional responsibilities for months:
Current value (Ten of Pentacles): You've become essential to multiple projects and departments. Your institutional knowledge and relationships make you valuable beyond your job description.
Obstacles (Seven of Swords): Your boss might try to deflect with promises about "next quarter" or "after the restructuring." Get specific commitments, not vague assurances.
Opportunities (Ace of Pentacles): Company just landed a major contract. Budget exists, and your contributions directly supported that success.
Strategy (Queen of Pentacles): Present this as a business case, not a personal request. Document your expanded responsibilities and quantify your impact.
Action Steps:
* Create a detailed list of additional responsibilities you've taken on
* Research market rates for your expanded role
* Schedule the conversation for after the new contract announcement
* Prepare specific examples of how your work contributed to company success
* Have a backup plan if they can't meet your salary request (additional vacation, flexible schedule, professional development budget)
She got a 15% raise and a title change. The key was framing it as recognizing reality rather than asking for a favor. The cards helped her approach it strategically instead of emotionally.
5. "Career Pivot Navigator" Spread
This eight-card spread is for major career transitionsŚchanging industries, going back to school, starting a business, or any situation where you're stepping off the established path into something new. It's designed to help you navigate the complexity of major change.
Position 1 (Center): Why you need to change The real reason you're considering this pivot. What's pushing you away from your current path?
Position 2 (Top): Your transferable assets Skills, experience, connections, and qualities you can take with you to the new direction.
Position 3 (Right): New direction potential What this career change could offer you. Opportunities, growth, fulfillment, challenges.
Position 4 (Bottom): What you're leaving behind Not just the obvious things, but also identity, expertise, professional relationships, financial security.
Position 5 (Left): Bridge building requirements What you need to develop, learn, or acquire to make this transition successfully.
Position 6 (Top left): Timeline and pacing How fast or slow to make this change. What to do first, what can wait.
Position 7 (Top right): Support and resources What help you need, where to find it, who to connect with.
Position 8 (Outside): Ultimate outcome Where this pivot could lead if executed well. The long-term vision.
Sample Reading:
I used this spread when considering leaving ICU nursing to focus more on tech and business work:
Why change (Death): The ICU work was literally killing parts of me I needed to preserve. Burnout, compassion fatigue, physical exhaustion.
Transferable assets (Queen of Pentacles): Project management skills, crisis decision-making, ability to work under pressure, healthcare industry knowledge.
New direction potential (The Star): Sustainable income doing work that energizes rather than drains me. Better work-life integration.
Leaving behind (Ten of Cups): Professional identity as "a nurse," immediate patient impact, clear career progression path, team camaraderie.
Bridge requirements (The Hermit): Need to develop business and technical skills, build professional network outside healthcare.
Timeline (Two of Pentacles): Gradual transition, maintaining some clinical work while building other income streams.
Support (Three of Pentacles): Find mentors in tech/business world, join professional organizations, invest in relevant education.
Ultimate outcome (The World): Integration of all my skills and interests into a career that serves others while supporting my well-being.
Action Steps:
* Reduce ICU hours to part-time while building other income
* Invest in coding courses and business education
* Network in tech and healthcare innovation spaces
* Set specific financial benchmarks for transition phases
* Maintain nursing license as safety net during transition
This reading helped me create a strategic transition plan instead of just quitting impulsively when the stress got overwhelming. Career pivots work better when they're planned gradually rather than executed dramatically.
6. "Burnout Assessment" Spread
This five-card spread is for when you think you might be experiencing work-related burnout but aren't sure if you're just having a bad week or if this is something more serious that requires intervention. It helps distinguish between temporary stress and systemic problems.
Position 1 (Center): Current energy state What's really going on with your relationship to work right now? Where are you mentally, emotionally, physically?
Position 2 (Top): External pressures What workplace factors are contributing to your stress? Workload, management, environment, changes.
Position 3 (Bottom): Internal factors How are your own patterns, expectations, or coping mechanisms contributing to the problem?
Position 4 (Left): What needs immediate attention The most urgent thing to address. What can't wait for a better time or more resources?
Position 5 (Right): Long-term restoration needs What has to change systemically for you to sustain this work long-term? Big picture solutions.
Sample Reading:
A nurse colleague pulled this spread during a particularly brutal stretch in the pandemic:
Current state (Nine of Swords): Mental exhaustion, anxiety about work even on days off, physical symptoms of chronic stress.
External pressures (Five of Wands): Understaffing, constantly changing protocols, management decisions that don't account for floor reality.
Internal factors (Eight of Swords): Perfectionist tendencies, inability to say no to extra shifts, guilt about patient care when exhausted.
Immediate attention (Four of Cups): Need to start declining overtime and extra responsibilities immediately, even if it feels selfish.
Long-term needs (Six of Pentacles): Either workplace conditions improve significantly, or she needs to find a different role. Current situation isn't sustainable.
Action Steps:
* Stop picking up extra shifts for at least two weeks
* Use Employee Assistance Program for counseling support
* Have honest conversation with manager about workload and sustainability
* Begin exploring other nursing roles with better work-life balance
* Set boundaries around checking work messages on days off
She ended up transferring to a different unit with better staffing ratios and management support. Sometimes burnout assessment reveals that the problem isn't youŚit's the situation, and the situation needs to change.

These spreads work because they force you to consider aspects of work decisions that you might otherwise ignore or rationalize away. They don't make decisions for you, but they help you make decisions from a more complete understanding of what you're actually dealing with.
The key is to use them when you have real decisions to make, not just when you're procrastinating or fantasizing about change. Pull the cards when you have actual interviews scheduled, concrete opportunities to evaluate, or specific problems that need addressing.
And rememberŚthe cards might tell you things you don't want to hear. They might reveal that your dream job has practical problems you haven't considered, or that staying in your current situation is actually the smart move right now, or that the change you need to make is different from the change you want to make.
That's not the cards being meanŚthat's the cards being useful. Because sometimes the most helpful thing someone can do is point out the reality you're trying not to see, so you can make decisions based on what's actually true instead of what you wish were true.
Your career is going to be a series of choices made with incomplete information under time pressure while other people have opinions about what you should do. The cards can't eliminate that complexity, but they can help you navigate it with more clarity and less second-guessing.
Which, honestly, is about as close to professional magic as any of us are going to get.






















PART VII: READING FOR DIVERSE EXPERIENCES 


Chapter 19: Neurodivergent Navigations: ADHD, Autism, and Archetypes
Look, we need to talk about something that most tarot books completely ignore: not everyone's brain processes information the same way. And if you're neurodivergentŚwhether that's ADHD, autism, or any of the other beautiful ways human brains can work differentlyŚtraditional tarot approaches might feel like they were designed by and for neurotypical people. Because, honestly, they probably were.
I've been reading cards for over two decades, and I've spent just as long working in healthcare, which means I've seen firsthand how often "one size fits all" approaches actually fit nobody particularly well. This is especially true when it comes to practices like tarot that rely heavily on pattern recognition, sensory input, focus, and intuitive processingŚall areas where neurodivergent brains might work differently, not worse, just differently.
The thing is, neurodivergent brains often have advantages when it comes to tarot reading. ADHD brains excel at seeing connections others miss, hyperfocusing on details, and thinking outside conventional frameworks. Autistic brains bring systematic thinking, pattern recognition abilities, and deep focus that can unlock layers of meaning others might overlook. But most tarot resources don't acknowledge these strengths or provide modifications that help neurodivergent readers access them.
So let's fix that. This chapter isn't about pathologizing different ways of thinking or providing "accommodations" like neurodivergence is something that needs to be worked around. It's about recognizing that different cognitive styles might connect with the cards differently, and that's actually a feature, not a bug.
ADHD Lens on Major Arcana
ADHD brains process the Major Arcana through a lens of executive function challenges, emotional intensity, and non-linear thinking patterns. If you have ADHD, you might find that certain cards resonate more strongly because they reflect your lived experience of navigating a world designed for different cognitive patterns.
The Fool is pure ADHD energyŚjumping into new projects with enthusiasm and no real plan, trusting that you'll figure it out as you go. This card represents the beautiful impulsivity that drives innovation and creativity, even when other people think you should "plan better" or "be more realistic." The Fool doesn't overthink; they act on inspiration, which is exactly how ADHD brains often work best.
When The Fool appears in readings, it might be validating that spontaneous decision you've been talking yourself out of, or reminding you that not everything needs a detailed plan to be worth pursuing. Sometimes the best adventures happen when you trust your instincts and figure out the logistics later.
The Magician speaks to the ADHD superpower of hyperfocus. When you're in flow state, when interest and stimulation align perfectly, you become capable of almost magical productivity and creativity. This card appears when you have all the tools you need to succeed, you just need to harness your focus and direct it intentionally.
For ADHD readers, The Magician often shows up as a reminder that you work best when you're genuinely interested in what you're doing. It's permission to find ways to make necessary tasks more engaging rather than forcing yourself through boring but "important" work that your brain simply won't cooperate with.
The High Priestess represents the intuitive knowing that ADHD brains excel atŚthe ability to sense patterns, read between lines, and pick up on subtleties that others miss. This card validates the gut feelings and "random" insights that often turn out to be surprisingly accurate.
The Emperor can be a challenging card for ADHD brains because it represents structure, discipline, and systematic approachesŚthings that don't always come naturally. But when this card appears, it might be suggesting that some structure would actually support your goals, or that you need to work with your brain's patterns rather than against them to create sustainable systems.
The Hierophant often represents the conventional approaches and traditional methods that ADHD brains find stifling. When this card appears, it might be highlighting the tension between "how things are supposed to be done" and how your brain actually works best. Sometimes it's a reminder to honor conventional wisdom; other times it's pointing out that conventional wisdom doesn't account for neurodivergent ways of thinking.
The Lovers for ADHD brains often highlights the challenge of choice and decision-making when you can see multiple possibilities and potential outcomes simultaneously. This card might appear when you're paralyzed by options or when you need to choose between equally appealing paths.
The Chariot represents successful management of competing impulses and directionsŚa key challenge for ADHD brains. This card appears when you need to harness your scattered energy and point it toward specific goals, or when you're successfully managing multiple projects without burning out.
Strength speaks to the inner discipline required to work with ADHD rather than against it. This isn't about forcing yourself to function like a neurotypical brain; it's about developing strategies that work with your natural patterns while building the stamina to persist through challenges.
The Hermit can represent the need for sensory breaks, quiet processing time, or stepping away from overstimulating environments to reconnect with your inner wisdom. For ADHD brains, this card often appears when you need to create space for reflection and recharging.
Wheel of Fortune reflects the cyclical nature of ADHD symptoms and energy levels. Good days and difficult days, periods of high productivity and times when everything feels impossible. This card reminds you that the difficult phases are temporary and the productive phases will return.
Justice speaks to the need for fair accommodations and working environments that support rather than hinder neurodivergent thinking. This card might appear when you need to advocate for yourself or when you're dealing with systems that don't account for different cognitive styles.
The Hanged Man perfectly captures the ADHD experience of feeling stuck because conventional approaches aren't working, and needing to find entirely different ways of approaching problems. This card validates the need to flip your perspective and find solutions that others might not understand but that work for your specific brain.
Death represents the transformation that happens when you stop trying to force yourself into neurotypical molds and start honoring how your brain actually works. This card often appears during the process of accepting neurodivergence and developing strategies that support rather than fight your natural patterns.
Temperance is about finding balance and integrationŚlearning to work with ADHD rather than constantly struggling against it. This card appears when you're developing sustainable approaches to managing executive function challenges while honoring your strengths.
The Devil can represent the addiction-like pull of hyperfocus, procrastination patterns, or unhealthy coping mechanisms for managing ADHD challenges. This card appears when these patterns are becoming problematic and need conscious intervention.
The Tower reflects the dramatic shifts that happen when masking or forcing neurotypical behavior becomes unsustainable. This card often appears during ADHD diagnosis or when major life changes require new approaches to managing executive function.
The Star represents hope and healing after accepting neurodivergence. This card appears when you're finding strategies that actually work, building environments that support your success, and connecting with communities that understand your experience.
The Moon speaks to the confusion and uncertainty that can accompany ADHD, especially around self-doubt, rejection sensitivity, and difficulty distinguishing between intuition and anxiety. This card appears when you need to trust your perceptions while also questioning patterns that might not be serving you.
The Sun represents the joy and vitality that emerge when you're working with your ADHD rather than against it. This card appears when you've found your flow, when your hyperfocus is channeled productively, when your creativity is supported and celebrated.
Judgment calls for honest assessment of what's working and what isn't in your approach to managing ADHD. This card appears when you need to release strategies that aren't serving you and embrace approaches that support your natural patterns.
The World represents integration and completionŚsuccessfully creating a life and work environment that supports neurodivergent thinking while achieving your goals. This card appears when you've found sustainable ways to honor both your ADHD and your aspirations.
Autism Lens on Major Arcana
Autistic brains process the Major Arcana through patterns of sensory sensitivity, social navigation challenges, intense interests, and systematic thinking. If you're autistic, certain cards might resonate more strongly because they reflect the unique strengths and challenges of autistic cognition.
The Fool for autistic brains represents the courage required to navigate social situations and new environments despite uncertainty about unwritten rules and expectations. This card validates the bravery of stepping into unfamiliar territory when you process social and sensory information differently.
The Magician speaks to the focused intensity that autistic brains bring to special interests and systematic approaches. When you're working within your areas of expertise and passion, you demonstrate almost magical levels of skill and knowledge. This card appears when you need to trust your specialized abilities and systematic thinking.
The High Priestess represents the deep pattern recognition abilities that many autistic brains possess. This card validates the intuitive understanding of systems, the ability to see connections others miss, and the capacity for profound insight when given adequate processing time.
The Empress can be complicated for autistic brains because it represents nurturing and creativity that might not look conventional. This card might appear when you need to honor your own ways of caring for others or expressing creativity, even if they don't match neurotypical expectations.
The Emperor often resonates strongly with autistic systematic thinking. This card represents structure, clear rules, and logical organizationŚthings that many autistic brains find comforting and productive. When this card appears, it might be validating your need for predictability and clear expectations.
The Hierophant represents both the comfort of established systems and traditions, and the frustration when those systems don't account for autistic ways of thinking. This card might appear when you're struggling with social institutions that expect neurotypical behavior patterns.
The Lovers for autistic brains often highlights the complexity of forming deep connections when social communication patterns are different. This card might appear when you're navigating relationships, whether romantic, platonic, or professional, and trying to bridge different communication styles.
The Chariot represents the determination and focus required to navigate a world that often isn't designed for autistic sensory and social needs. This card appears when you're successfully managing competing demands while honoring your own processing requirements.
Strength speaks to the inner resilience required for masking, social navigation, and managing sensory overwhelm while pursuing your goals. This card validates the tremendous strength it takes to function in environments that aren't naturally supportive of autistic cognition.
The Hermit often represents the necessary retreat and recovery time that autistic brains need after social or sensory overload. This card validates the need for solitude, quiet processing time, and environments that don't overwhelm your sensory systems.
Wheel of Fortune reflects the cyclical nature of autistic energy and capacity. Periods of high functioning and productivity followed by times when everything feels overwhelming and you need to reduce demands. This card reminds you that these cycles are natural and temporary.
Justice speaks to the need for fair treatment, reasonable accommodations, and social systems that account for neurodivergent needs. This card might appear when you're advocating for yourself or dealing with discrimination based on different communication or behavioral patterns.
The Hanged Man captures the experience of being suspended between autistic authenticity and social expectations. This card appears when you're questioning whether to mask or be authentic, or when you need to find entirely different approaches to challenges that conventional wisdom doesn't address.
Death represents the transformation that happens when you stop forcing yourself into neurotypical behavioral patterns and start honoring autistic ways of being. This card often appears during late-in-life autism diagnosis or when you're learning to unmask.
Temperance is about finding balance between honoring autistic needs and functioning in a neurotypical world. This card appears when you're developing sustainable approaches to social interaction, sensory management, and emotional regulation.
The Devil can represent unhealthy masking patterns, burnout from trying to appear neurotypical, or becoming trapped in rigid thinking patterns that no longer serve you. This card appears when these patterns need conscious examination and change.
The Tower reflects the dramatic shifts that happen when masking becomes unsustainable or when autism diagnosis completely reframes your understanding of yourself. This card often appears during major life transitions that require new approaches to self-advocacy and environmental management.
The Star represents hope and healing after accepting autism as a different way of being rather than a deficit. This card appears when you're finding communities that understand you, developing strategies that honor your needs, and building environments that support autistic thriving.
The Moon speaks to the confusion that can accompany late diagnosis, the difficulty of distinguishing between intuition and social anxiety, and the challenge of trusting your perceptions when others consistently misunderstand your communication.
The Sun represents the joy and vitality that emerge when you're able to be authentically autistic in supportive environments. This card appears when your special interests are celebrated, your systematic thinking is valued, and your sensory needs are accommodated.
Judgment calls for honest assessment of masking patterns, social expectations you've internalized, and strategies that support versus exhaust you. This card appears when you need to release approaches that aren't sustainable and embrace authenticity.
The World represents integration and wholenessŚsuccessfully creating a life that honors autistic strengths while meeting your goals and values. This card appears when you've found sustainable ways to be authentically yourself while building meaningful connections and pursuing your interests.
Executive Function Spreads
Executive function challenges affect many neurodivergent people, but they also impact anyone dealing with stress, trauma, depression, or major life transitions. These spreads are designed to help you understand and work with executive function patterns rather than judging them as personal failures.
"Why Can't I Get Started?" Spread
This four-card spread addresses initiation difficultiesŚwhen you know what you need to do but can't seem to begin.
Position 1: The real barrier What's actually preventing initiation? Perfectionism, overwhelm, unclear priorities, lack of interest, executive dysfunction?
Position 2: Hidden resistance What part of this task or situation is your brain trying to protect you from? Sometimes procrastination is actually wisdom about problematic demands or expectations.
Position 3: Energy and capacity What does your current mental and emotional state have available for this task? Are you trying to push through when you need rest?
Position 4: Alternative approach How could you modify the task, environment, or expectations to work with your brain rather than against it?
Sample Reading: Someone struggling to start job applications pulled:
* Real barrier (Seven of Swords): Fear of rejection was making every application feel like setting yourself up for failure
* Hidden resistance (Four of Pentacles): Holding onto current situation because job searching felt like admitting the current role wasn't working
* Energy capacity (Five of Pentacles): Burnout was making everything feel impossible; needed recovery time before tackling job search
* Alternative approach (Three of Pentacles): Focus on networking and informational interviews instead of formal applications; make it collaborative rather than isolating
"Task Switching Troubles" Spread
This three-card spread addresses difficulty transitioning between activitiesŚa common executive function challenge.
Position 1: What makes transitions hard Hyperfocus, anxiety about the next task, sensory overwhelm, unclear priorities?
Position 2: Transition support needed What would help you move between activities more smoothly? Time buffers, environmental changes, energy management?
Position 3: Flow optimization How could you organize your day or tasks to minimize difficult transitions and maximize natural flow?
"Overwhelm Management" Spread
This five-card spread helps when everything feels like too much and you can't figure out where to start.
Position 1: Source of overwhelm Too many tasks, unclear priorities, perfectionist expectations, external pressures, internal criticism?
Position 2: What can wait Tasks or expectations that aren't actually urgent despite feeling overwhelming.
Position 3: What needs immediate attention The one or two things that actually require action now.
Position 4: Support systems What help is available? People, tools, accommodations, environmental changes?
Position 5: Recovery needs What does your nervous system need to regulate before tackling tasks? Rest, movement, social connection, sensory comfort?
Sensory Considerations for Reading
Traditional tarot often ignores how sensory processing differences affect the reading experience. But for many people, especially those with autism, ADHD, or sensory processing sensitivity, the physical experience of tarot reading is just as important as the interpretive process.
Visual Processing Considerations
Some people process visual information differently, which can affect how they connect with tarot imagery. If you're easily overwhelmed by busy visual designs, consider decks with simpler, cleaner artwork. The Rider-Waite-Smith imagery can be overwhelming for some visual processors, while minimalist decks might not provide enough visual information for others.
Pay attention to color sensitivity. Bright, high-contrast decks might be overstimulating for some readers, while others need that level of visual intensity to focus properly. Some people find black-and-white or monochromatic decks easier to process, while others need full-color imagery to engage with the cards.
Lighting matters more than most people realize. Fluorescent lighting can cause visual strain and make it harder to focus on card details. Natural light or warm LED lighting often works better for extended reading sessions. If you're reading in low light, make sure you have adequate illumination to reduce eye strain.
Card size can affect visual processing. Some people find standard tarot cards too small to comfortably see details, while others find large cards overwhelming or difficult to handle. Mini decks work well for some visual processors but might be too small for others to engage with effectively.
Tactile Processing Considerations
The physical sensation of cards matters, especially for readers who process information kinesthetically. Some people need the substantial feel of thick, high-quality cardstock to connect with the cards, while others prefer thinner, more flexible cards that are easier to shuffle.
Texture affects the reading experience. Smooth, glossy cards feel different from matte finishes, and some people have strong preferences or sensitivities. If you're tactilely sensitive, pay attention to how different card finishes feel in your hands and choose accordingly.
Shuffling styles need to accommodate different motor skills and sensory preferences. Traditional riffle shuffling might be difficult for people with fine motor challenges or joint hypermobility. Overhand shuffling, wash shuffling, or simply stirring cards on a table can be more accessible options.
Card edges matter for comfort. Sharp-edged cards can be uncomfortable for extended handling, especially if you have sensory sensitivities. Rounded corners are more comfortable for many people and reduce the risk of card damage from frequent handling.
Auditory Processing Considerations
Environmental noise affects concentration differently for different brains. Some people need complete silence to focus on readings, while others concentrate better with background noise or music. Sudden or unpredictable sounds can be particularly disruptive for people with auditory sensitivities.
If you're reading for others, pay attention to how your voice and their voice affect the reading experience. Some people process spoken information better than visual information, while others find talking disruptive to their interpretive process.
The sound of shuffling and handling cards provides auditory feedback that some readers rely on. The rhythm of shuffling can be calming and help with focus, while the sound of cards being placed can provide confirmation of intention and choice.
Creating Sensory-Supportive Reading Environments
Temperature and air quality affect cognitive function more than most people realize. Stuffy, overheated rooms can make concentration difficult, while cold environments might cause physical tension that interferes with intuitive processing.
Comfortable seating supports longer reading sessions. If you're physically uncomfortable, it's harder to focus on cards and interpretation. This might mean reading in a chair with good back support, on the floor with cushions, or standing at a counterŚwhatever works for your body.
Fidget tools can support focus for people who need sensory input while thinking. Stress balls, fidget cubes, or textured stones can provide the sensory stimulation that helps some brains concentrate on cognitive tasks.
Scent can affect mood and concentration. Some people find incense or essential oils supportive for reading, while others find any artificial scents overwhelming or distracting. Pay attention to how different scents affect your ability to focus and interpret cards.
Time of day matters for cognitive function. Some people read more intuitively in the morning when their minds are fresh, while others connect better with cards in the evening when they're more relaxed. Honor your natural energy rhythms rather than forcing readings when your brain isn't cooperative.
Adapting Reading Practices for Different Processing Styles
Linear processors might prefer to read cards in order from left to right or top to bottom, while non-linear processors might jump around the spread following their attention and interest. Neither approach is better; they're just different ways of taking in information.
Processing time varies significantly between individuals. Some people need to look at cards for several minutes before interpretation emerges, while others get immediate impressions. Allow whatever time you need without judging your pace against others.
Note-taking can support memory and processing for some readers, while others find writing disruptive to their intuitive flow. Experiment with different ways of capturing insightsŚwritten notes, voice memos, photos of spreads, or simply trusting your memory.
Multiple processing modalities can enhance understanding. Some people benefit from reading cards silently, others from speaking interpretations aloud, and still others from moving while they think or drawing connections between cards.
The goal isn't to force yourself into standard tarot practices that don't work for your brain. It's to adapt the practice to support your natural processing patterns while still accessing the wisdom and insight that tarot can provide. Your neurodivergent brain isn't a limitation to work aroundŚit's a different way of thinking that can bring unique perspectives to card interpretation and spiritual practice.
Remember that sensory and cognitive differences aren't deficits requiring accommodationŚthey're variations in human experience that can offer valuable insights and approaches to tarot reading. The cards don't care if you process them differently; they care that you're engaging authentically with the process and allowing their wisdom to speak to your specific experience and needs.


CHAPTER 20: CHRONIC ILLNESS CHRONICLES: LONG-TERM CARD COMPANIONS
Let me tell you something about chronic illness and tarot that nobody else is going to say this directly: when you're dealing with a long-term health condition, most traditional tarot advice becomes useless real quick. All those spreads about "manifesting your dreams" and "following your passion" hit different when your body has its own agenda that doesn't give a damn about your five-year plan.
I've been working in healthcare for over two decades, and I've spent just as long reading cards for people navigating everything from autoimmune conditions to chronic pain, from mental health diagnoses to progressive neurological disorders. What I've learned is that chronic illness requires a completely different approach to tarotŚone that accounts for the reality of energy limitations, unpredictable symptom patterns, medical decision fatigue, and the ongoing process of integrating illness into identity.
The cards don't care if you're having a flare-up. They don't adjust their wisdom for your current prednisone dose or factor in your latest lab results. But they can become invaluable tools for navigating the complex reality of living in a body that requires constant negotiation, medical management, and adaptive strategies.
This isn't about using tarot to "heal yourself" or bypass medical treatment. That's dangerous bullshit that belongs nowhere near actual illness management. This is about using cards as decision-making tools, energy management guides, and identity integration support while you're doing the real work of managing chronic conditions with actual medical care.
Energy Accounting with Cards
When you live with chronic illness, energy becomes currency. Not the mystical "good vibes" energy that wellness culture talks about, but actual metabolic energy, cognitive capacity, emotional reserves, and physical stamina. These are finite resources that require careful budgeting, and traditional productivity culture has no fucking clue how to account for this reality.
The Suit of Pentacles becomes your energy accounting system when you're dealing with chronic illness. Each card represents different aspects of energy expenditure, conservation, and investment that healthy people take for granted but chronically ill people have to plan strategically.
Ace of Pentacles represents available energy potential on any given day. This isn't about manifestation; it's about honest assessment. What does your body actually have to offer today? What are your cognitive resources? What's your pain baseline? This card appears when you need to take accurate inventory before making commitments or plans.
I use this card check-in most mornings, not as some mystical ritual, but as a practical assessment tool. If I pull the Ace upright, my system feels stable enough for normal activities. Reversed? Time to adjust expectations and plan for lower energy output. It's no different from checking your blood glucose levels or monitoring blood pressureŚdata collection for better self-management.
Two of Pentacles is the chronic illness juggling act. Managing medication schedules, appointment logistics, symptom tracking, insurance battles, work accommodations, and family responsibilities while dealing with fluctuating capacity. This card validates how complex chronic illness management actually is and reminds you that the juggling itself is skilled work.
Three of Pentacles represents the collaborative nature of chronic illness care. Your medical team, support network, workplace accommodations, family members, and other people with similar conditions. This card appears when you need to lean on your support systems or when coordination between different aspects of your care is required.
Four of Pentacles shows up when illness anxiety makes you hoard energy or become overly protective of resources. Sometimes this is appropriateŚsaving energy for essential activities. But sometimes it becomes limiting, keeping you from engaging in activities that might actually support your well-being. This card asks you to examine whether your energy conservation is strategic or fear-based.
Five of Pentacles is the resource scarcity that often accompanies chronic illness. Medical expenses, reduced income from missed work, the cost of accommodations and adaptive equipment, insurance battles. This card validates the financial stress that chronic illness creates while reminding you that this situation is temporary and support exists.
Six of Pentacles represents the complex dance of giving and receiving that chronic illness requires. Accepting help when you need it, offering support when you're able, navigating the guilt and gratitude that come with dependent relationships. This card appears when balance in relationships requires attention.
Seven of Pentacles is about patience with slow progress and long-term investment in health management. Chronic illness treatment rarely provides immediate results. This card appears when you're questioning whether your current treatment approach is working or when you need patience with gradual improvement.
Eight of Pentacles represents the expertise you develop in managing your own condition. Learning to read your body's signals, mastering medication timing, developing pain management strategies, becoming fluent in medical terminology. This card validates the skill and knowledge that chronic illness management requires.
Nine of Pentacles is about finding stability and security despite ongoing health challenges. This card appears when you've developed sustainable routines, effective treatment protocols, and adaptive strategies that allow for independence and quality of life within the constraints of chronic illness.
Ten of Pentacles represents long-term security and legacy despite chronic illness. Building wealth despite medical expenses, creating stability for family, developing expertise that helps others with similar conditions. This card appears when chronic illness becomes integrated into a larger life vision rather than dominating it.
The Court Cards in Pentacles represent different approaches to energy management:
Page of Pentacles is the newly diagnosed person learning to navigate chronic illness systems. Eager to understand their condition, sometimes overwhelmed by information, developing new skills for self-advocacy and medical communication.
Knight of Pentacles is the methodical approach to chronic illness management. Consistent with treatment protocols, thorough with documentation, steady progress toward health goals. Sometimes slow but ultimately reliable.
Queen of Pentacles is mastery of chronic illness self-care. Balancing medical needs with other life priorities, creating supportive environments, nurturing others while maintaining boundaries around energy.
King of Pentacles represents expertise and leadership in chronic illness communities. Using experience to help others, advocating for systemic changes, building resources and support networks.
Medical Decision Spreads
Medical decision-making with chronic illness involves complex risk-benefit analyses, quality of life considerations, and uncertainty management that most people never have to navigate. These spreads help organize thinking around medical choices without replacing professional medical advice.
"Treatment Option Analysis" Spread
This five-card spread helps evaluate medical treatments, procedures, or management strategies when you're facing complex decisions with multiple variables.
Position 1 (Center): Current baseline Your current symptom level, quality of life, functional capacity, and overall health status. This establishes what you're comparing other options against.
Position 2 (Left): Benefits of proposed treatment Potential improvements, symptom relief, functional gains, long-term health outcomes. What could you gain from this approach?
Position 3 (Right): Risks and side effects Potential complications, adverse reactions, quality of life impacts, financial costs. What could you lose or what problems might emerge?
Position 4 (Top): Timeline and expectations How long before you see results? What does the treatment trajectory look like? Are improvements temporary or permanent?
Position 5 (Bottom): Alternative considerations Other treatment options, complementary approaches, what happens if you delay decision-making. What else should you be considering?
Sample Reading:
A friend with rheumatoid arthritis used this spread when considering biologic therapy:
Current baseline (Five of Pentacles): Significant joint pain limiting work capacity, relationship strain from activity limitations, depression from chronic pain.
Treatment benefits (Six of Wands): Potential for significant symptom improvement, return to previous activity levels, renewed hope and energy.
Risks (The Tower): Immunosuppression risks, potential serious infections, expensive treatment with insurance uncertainty.
Timeline (Seven of Pentacles): Three to six months before seeing full benefits, ongoing treatment required, regular monitoring necessary.
Alternatives (The Hermit): Continue current medications with lifestyle modifications, consider different biologic options, explore integrative approaches.
Action Steps:
* Detailed discussion with rheumatologist about infection risk management
* Financial consultation with insurance company about long-term coverage
* Six-month trial period with specific functional goals
* Backup plan if treatment isn't effective or side effects emerge
"Medical Team Navigation" Spread
This four-card spread helps when you're dealing with multiple specialists, conflicting medical opinions, or communication challenges with healthcare providers.
Position 1: What you need from medical care Your priorities, concerns, and goals for treatment. What outcomes matter most to you?
Position 2: Current team dynamics How well your medical providers are communicating with each other and with you. Where are the gaps or conflicts?
Position 3: Advocacy strategy How to communicate effectively with your medical team, what questions to ask, how to ensure your voice is heard.
Position 4: Decision-making clarity What information you need to make informed decisions, whose opinion carries most weight, how to resolve conflicting recommendations.
Pacing Strategies
Pacing with chronic illness means learning to work within energy limitations rather than pushing through them. It's about sustainable activity management, not just "taking breaks." The cards can help develop and maintain pacing strategies that prevent boom-bust cycles.
The Hermit is your pacing guide card. This isn't about social isolation; it's about turning inward to assess your current capacity and needs. The Hermit asks: What does your body need right now? What pace supports your energy rather than depleting it? When do you need to step back and recalibrate?
In practical terms, The Hermit appears when you're pushing too hard and need to reassess your current approach to activity management. It's permission to slow down, reduce commitments, and prioritize rest without guilt.
Temperance represents the balance and moderation that effective pacing requires. This card appears when you need to find middle ground between doing nothing and overdoing everything. Temperance asks: How can you stay engaged with life while respecting your energy limitations?
Four of Swords is about strategic rest and recovery. This isn't lazy; it's medicine. Rest is a treatment modality for chronic illness, not a luxury or failure. This card appears when you need permission to prioritize recovery or when you're feeling guilty about resting.
Ten of Wands warns about overcommitment and the chronic illness pattern of trying to do everything when you're feeling good, then crashing when your energy runs out. This card appears when you need to delegate, ask for help, or reduce your load before you hit a wall.
The Wheel of Fortune acknowledges the cyclical nature of chronic illness symptoms. Good days and bad days, flares and remissions, periods of stability and times of increased symptoms. This card reminds you that both difficult and easier phases are temporary.
"Energy Budget Planning" Spread
This seven-card spread helps plan activities and commitments based on realistic energy assessment.
Position 1: Base energy level Your current overall capacity considering symptoms, medications, sleep quality, stress levels.
Position 2: Essential commitments Non-negotiable activities like medical appointments, work requirements, basic self-care.
Position 3: Discretionary activities Social plans, hobbies, optional tasks that enhance quality of life but aren't mandatory.
Position 4: Energy drains Activities, people, or situations that deplete your resources more than expected.
Position 5: Energy supports What activities, environments, or people actually restore rather than drain your energy.
Position 6: Flexibility needs How much buffer time and adaptability your schedule requires for symptom fluctuations.
Position 7: Integration strategy How to balance all these factors into a sustainable weekly or monthly rhythm.
"Flare Management" Spread
This three-card spread is for when symptoms are exacerbated and you need to adjust your approach quickly.
Position 1: Immediate priorities What absolutely must be done versus what can wait until symptoms improve.
Position 2: Support mobilization What help you need and where to find it. Who to call, what accommodations to request.
Position 3: Recovery strategy What your body needs to return to baseline. Rest requirements, medication adjustments, environmental modifications.
Identity Integration
Chronic illness forces identity reconstruction in ways that healthy people rarely have to navigate. You're no longer just "a teacher" or "a parent" or "an athlete"Śyou become "a teacher with fibromyalgia" or "a parent managing Crohn's disease" or "a former athlete living with multiple sclerosis." The cards can help navigate this complex identity integration process.
Death is the identity transformation card for chronic illness. Parts of your pre-illness identity may need to die for new, more sustainable versions to emerge. This isn't about giving up or becoming lesserŚit's about releasing unrealistic expectations and discovering new ways of being yourself.
Death appears when you're holding onto pre-illness capabilities or identities that no longer serve you. The marathon runner who becomes a walker. The workaholic who learns to prioritize health over productivity. The independent person who learns to accept help. These aren't failures; they're adaptations.
The Star represents hope and renewal after the identity crisis that chronic illness often precipitates. This card appears when you're finding new sources of meaning, discovering unexpected strengths, or connecting with communities that understand your experience.
The Hanged Man captures the suspension between old and new identities. You're not who you used to be, but you're not yet sure who you're becoming. This card validates the discomfort of identity limbo while suggesting that this suspended state is temporary and necessary for growth.
Justice speaks to the need for fairness and accommodation in how chronic illness affects your life and relationships. This card appears when you're advocating for workplace accommodations, fighting insurance denials, or setting boundaries with people who don't understand your limitations.
